Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Khul' on Unspecified Property: 'What is in My Hand'
Ruling with proof on khul' with unspecified compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife seeks khul' on unspecified property (like saying 'what is in my hand') and determine whether she must return the dower or complete a named amount.

Core Idea
If the wife seeks khul' on unspecified property and has nothing, she returns the dower; if she names an amount and has less, she completes it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the default rules when a wife initiates khul' without specifying exact compensation. The key is the interpretation of her statement 'on what is in my hand' and the consequences when nothing or insufficient property exists. Understanding this helps prevent disputes over vague compensation terms.

Guided Path
1
Interpret the wife's statement
Analyze her phrase 'on what is in my hand': it is vague but becomes specified by context; dirhams are an example, not exclusive.
The word 'ma' (what) is ambiguous; it is clarified by the context of dirhams etc.
2
Determine if anything is in her hand
If nothing is in her hand, the default is that she must return the dower she received. If she named a specific number (e.g., three dirhams) and has less, she must complete it to that number.
Condition: absence of property triggers dower return; absence of named amount triggers completion.
3
Apply the gross ignorance rule
If the compensation is so vague that it leads to gross ignorance (e.g., 'what the palm tree yields this year' with no fruit), the naming is invalid and she returns what she named and took (i.e., the dower or whatever was agreed).
Gross ignorance nullifies the specific naming, reverting to the dower.
4
Consider the nature of compensation
If the compensation is non-mutaqawwim (not legally valuable), nothing is due. Otherwise, the named valuable item is owed.
This ensures only property with legal value is enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الخلع. جمع درهم يصدق القرينة هنا على العهد وهو قولها على ما في يدي؛ كذا في الكافي. وأوضحه في فتح القدير فقال : لأن قولها على ما في يدي أفاد كون المسمى مظروفاً بيدها وهو عام يص على الدراهم وغيرها فصار بالدراهم عهد في الجملة من حيث هو مما صدقات لفظ ما وهو مبهم وقعت «من» بياناً له ومدخولها هو المبين لخصوص المظروف والدراهم مثال، والمراد أنها بينت المبهم بجمع كالدنانير وينبغي أن يكون قولها على ما في هذا البيت من الشياه أو الخيل أو البغال أو الحمير كذلك يلزمها ثلاثة من المسمى ثم رأيت في المعراج : لكن زاد الثياب وفيه نظر للجهالة المتفاحشة وقيد بقوله ولا شيء في يدها» لأنه لو كان في يدها مال متقوم كان له قليلاً كان أو كثيراً ولا يلزمها رد المهر في الأولى، وأما في الثانية فلا بد أن يكون في يدها مما سمته، فلو كان في يدها أو درهمان لزمها تكملة الثلاثة؛ كذا في الخانية والمبسوط. وبهذا علم أن في كلام المصنف مسامحة لأن عدم وجود شيء في يدها شرط لرد المهر في الأولى، وعدم وجود الثلاثة شرط في الثانية، وكلامه لا يفيده. وأفاد بقوله ردت المهر أنه مقبوض فيدل على أنه لو لم يكن مقبوضاً بريء منه ولا شيء عليها كما ذكره العمادي في فصوله وفي الجوهرة ثم إذا وجب الرجوع بالمهر له وكانت قد أبرأته لم يرجع عليها بشيء لأن عين ما يستحقه قد سلم له بالبراءة فلو رجع عليها لأجل الهبة وهي لا توجب على الواهب ضماناً .اهـ. وفي البزازية: والحاصل أنه إذا يرجع ما ليس بمتقوم لا يجب شيء، وإن سمى موجوداً معلوماً يجب المسمى، وإن سمى مجهولاً جهالة مستدركة فكذلك وإن فحشت الجهالة وتمكن الخطر بأن خالعها على ما يثمر نخلها العام أو على ما في البيت من المتاع ولم يكن فيه شيء بطلت التسمية وردت ما سمي منه قبضت اهـ. وقيد بالخلع لأن السيد لو أعتق عبده على ما في يده من الدراهم وليس في يده شيء يجب عليه قيمة نفسه لأن منافع البضع غير متقومة حالة الخروج فلا يشترط كون المسمى معلوماً بخلاف العبد فإنه متقوم في نفسه، وبخلاف النكاح حيث يجب مهر المثل لأنه متقوم حالة الدخول؛ كذا في البدائع. ودلت المسألة الأولى على أنه لو خالعها على عبد بعينه مثلاً وقد كان ميتاً قبل الخلع أنه يرجع عليها بالمهر الذي أخذته منه للغرور بخلاف ما لو مات بعده حيث تجب قيمته كما لو استحق وظهور حريته كموته قبل الخلع فيرجع عليها بالمهر قوله : ( وفيه نظر للجهالة المتفاحشة قال في النهر : ينبغي إيجاب الوسط في الكل وبه يندفع ما قال ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأن إيجاب الوسط في معلوم الجنس كالفرس والثوب الهروي بخلاف مجهول الجنس كالدابة والثوب ولذا لو سمى مهراً وجب مهر المثل قوله : وبهذا علم إن في كلام المصنف مسامحة الخ) قال في النهر : نفي الشيئية فيما إذا لم تسم له شيئاً معناه نفي الوجود، وفيها إذا سمت دراهم فإن خالع على عبد أبق لها على أنها بريئة من ضمانه لم تبرأ قالت طلقني ثلاثاً مهر عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف بقيمته لو كان عبداً كالمهر وقتله عنده بسبب كان عندها كاستحقاقه فيرجع بقيمته، كذا لو قطع يده؛ كذا في المبسوط. وأشار بقوله ردت المهر» إلى صحة الخلع على المهر وقد قال في الجوهرة وإن وقع الخلع على المهر صح فإن لم تقبضه المرأة سقط عنه وإن قبضته استرده منها .اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : خلعها بما لها عليه من المهر ظناً منه أن لها عليه بقية المهر ثم تذكر أنه لم يبق عليه شيء من المهر وقع الطلاق بمهرها فيجب عليها أن ترد المهر لأنه طلقها بطمع ما بقي عليه فلا يقع مجاناً، أما إذا علم أن لا لها عليه فلا شيء له اهـ. وفي القنية : ادعت مهرها على زوجها فأنكره ثم اختعلت نفسها بمهرها وقبل ثم تبين بالشهود أنها كانت أبرأته قبل الخلع فليس له شيء، ولو اختلعت على عبد ثم تبين أنه عبد الزوج ولا يعلم ذلك إلا بالتصادق ينبغي أن لا يلزمها شيء لأن ما هو بدل الخلع مسلم له كما لو علم أنه عبده قوله : (فإن خالعها على عبد أبق لها على أنها برية من ضمانه لم تبرأ لأنه عقد معاوضة فيقتضي سلامة العوض واشتراط البراءة شرط فاسد فبطل فكان عليها تسليم عينه إن قدرت وتسليم قيمته إن عجزت أشار إلى أن الخلع لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالنكاح ولذا قال في العمادي: لو خالعها على أن يمسك الولد عنده صح الخلع وبطل الشرط اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو اختلعت من زوجها على أن جعلت صداقها لولدها أو على أن تجعل صداقها لفلان الأجنبي قال محمد : الخلع جائز والمهر للزوج ولا شيء للولد ولا للأجنبي اهـ. ومعنى اشتراطها البراءة أنها إن وجدته سلمته وإلا فلا شيء عليها. وقيد باشتراط البراءة من ضمانه لأنها لو اشترطت البراءة من عيب في البدل صح الشرط وإنما صحت تسمية الآبق في الخلع لأن مبناه على المسامحة بخلاف البيع لأن مبناه على المضايقة، فالعجز عن التسليم يفضي إلى المنازعة فيه ولا كذلك هنا لأن العجز عن التسليم هنا دون العجز عن التسليم فيما إذا اختلعت على عبد الغير أو على ما في بطن غنمها وذلك جائز فكذا هنا وقيد بالشرط الفاسد لأن الشرط لو كان ملائماً لم يبطل ولذا قال في القنية : خالعها على ثوب بشرط أن تسلم إليه الثوب فقبلت فهلك الثوب قبل التسليم لم تبن لأنه يجعل نفس التسليم شرطاً. وهبت مهرها لأخيها فأخذ أخوها منه المهر قبالة ثم اختلعت نفسها منه بشرط أن تسلم إليه القبالة غداً فقبل ولم تسلم إليه القبالة غداً لم تحرم. ولو اختلعت بشرط الصك أو قالت بشرط أن يرد إليها أقمشتها فقبل لا تحرم ويشترط كتبه الصك مالاً أو دراهم معناه نفي وجود ما سمته وعلى هذا فلا مسامحة أصلاً إلا أن مقتضاه أنها لو سمت دراهم فإذا في يدها دنانير أنه لا يجب له غير الدراهم ولم أره. قوله : (ولذا قال في القنية تقدمت هذه العبارة قريباً قبيل قوله «فإن خالعها».
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. A group of dirhams is believed; the indication here is on the covenant, which is her saying 'on what is in my hand.' Thus in al-Kafi. And he clarified it in Fath al-Qadir, saying: Because her saying 'on what is in my hand' implies that the named thing is a container in her hand, and it is general, applying to dirhams and others, so dirhams became a covenant in general, because it is part of what the word 'what' indicates, which is vague. 'Min' was made an explication of it, and its object is the specific explanation of the container, and dirhams are an example. The intended meaning is that she explained the vague thing with a collective, like dinars. And it should be that her saying 'on what is in this house of sheep, horses, mules, or donkeys' is likewise, so three of the named thing are binding upon her. Then I saw in al-Mi'raj: but he added clothes, and there is a consideration due to gross ignorance. And he restricted by his saying 'and there is nothing in her hand', because if there was valuable wealth in her hand, it is for him, little or much, and she is not obligated to return the dower in the first case. As for the second, it is necessary that there be in her hand something of what she named. So if there was in her hand one or two dirhams, she must complete three. Thus in al-Khaniya and al-Mabsut. And by this it is known that there is a leniency in the author's words, because the absence of anything in her hand is a condition for returning the dower in the first case, and the absence of three is a condition in the second, and his words do not convey that. And his saying 'she returned the dower' indicates that it was received, so it indicates that if it had not been received, he is free of it and nothing is upon her, as al-Imadi mentioned in his chapters and in al-Jawhara. Then if recourse to the dower is due to him and she had absolved him, he does not recourse against her for anything, because the very thing he deserves has been delivered to him by the absolution; if he had recourse against her due to a gift, it does not obligate the donor a guarantee. End quote. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The conclusion is that if he returns what is not valuable, nothing is due; if he named an existing known thing, the named thing is due; if he named an unknown thing with remediable ignorance, likewise; and if the ignorance is gross and risk occurs, such as if he divorces her on what her palm tree yields this year or on what is in the house of goods and there is nothing in it, the naming is invalidated and she returns what she named and took of it. End quote. And it is restricted to khul' because if a master frees his slave on what is in his hand of dirhams and there is nothing in his hand, the value of himself is due upon him, because the usufruct of the vulva is not valuable at the time of exit, so it is not required that the named thing be known, unlike the slave, for he is valuable in himself, and unlike marriage, where the fair dower is due because it is valuable at the time of entry. Thus in al-Bada'i. And the first issue indicates that if he divorced her by khul' on a specific slave, for example, and it was dead before the khul', he recourses against her for the dower she took from him due to deception, unlike if he died after, where its value is due, just as if it was claimed and its freedom became apparent, like its death before khul', so he recourses against her for the dower. His saying: (And there is a consideration due to gross ignorance) He said in al-Nahr: It is appropriate to obligate the middle in all, and by it what he said is repelled. End quote. And there is a consideration because obligating the middle is for a known species like a horse and a Herawi cloth, unlike an unknown species like an animal and a cloth. That is why if he named a dower, the fair dower is due. His saying: And by this it is known that there is a leniency in the author's words, etc. He said in al-Nahr: The negation of thing-ness when she did not name him anything means negation of existence; and in it, if she named dirhams, then if he divorces her by khul' on an absconding slave of hers on condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free. She said: 'Divorce me three times for a dower' according to them, and according to Abu Yusuf, for its value if it was a slave like the dower, and his killing by him due to a cause while it was with her like its being claimed, he recourses for its value, similarly if he cut its hand. Thus in al-Mabsut. And his saying 'she returned the dower' indicates the validity of khul' on the dower, and he said in al-Jawhara: If khul' occurs on the dower, it is valid; if the woman did not take it, it falls from him; and if she took it, he retrieves it from her. End quote. And in al-Walwaljiyya: She divorced herself by khul' for the dower that he owed her, thinking that he owed her part of the dower, then he remembered that nothing of the dower remained upon him; the divorce occurs for her dower, so she must return the dower, because he divorced her with the hope of what remained upon him, so it does not occur for free. But if he knew that nothing was due to her, then nothing is due to him. End quote. And in al-Qunyah: She claimed her dower from her husband and he denied it, then she divorced herself by khul' for her dower, and he accepted, then it became clear by witnesses that she had absolved him before the khul'; he has nothing. And if she divorced herself by khul' on a slave, then it became clear that it was the husband's slave, and it is not known except by mutual confirmation, it is recommended that nothing be due from her, because what is the substitute of khul' is surrendered to him, just as if it was known that it was his slave. His saying: (For if he divorces her by khul' on an absconding slave of hers on condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free) because it is a contract of exchange, so it requires safety of the substitute, and stipulating freedom is a void condition, so it fails, so she must deliver its identical if able, and deliver its value if unable. He indicated that khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, like marriage. That is why he said in al-Imadi: If he divorces her by khul' on condition that he keep the child with him, the khul' is valid and the condition is void. End quote. And in al-Khaniya: If she divorced herself from her husband on condition that she made her dower for her child, or on condition that she made her dower for a stranger, Muhammad said: The khul' is valid and the dower is for the husband, and nothing for the child or the stranger. End quote. And the meaning of her stipulating freedom is that if she finds him, she delivers him; otherwise, nothing is upon her. And it is restricted by stipulating freedom from its guarantee, because if she stipulated freedom from a defect in the substitute, the condition is valid. And the naming of the absconding slave is valid in khul' because its basis is lenience, unlike sale because its basis is stringency. For inability to deliver leads to dispute in sale, not here, because inability to deliver here is less than inability to deliver when she divorces herself on a slave belonging to another or on what is in the belly of her sheep, which is permissible, so here likewise. And it is restricted by the void condition, because if the condition was appropriate, it would not be void. That is why he said in al-Qunyah: He divorced her by khul' on a cloth on condition that she deliver the cloth to him, and she accepted, then the cloth perished before delivery; she does not become prohibited, because he makes delivery itself a condition. And she gifted her dower to her brother, so her brother took from him the dower as a written promise, then she divorced herself by khul' on condition that she deliver the written promise to him tomorrow, and he accepted, and she did not deliver the written promise tomorrow; she does not become prohibited. And if she divorces herself by khul' with the condition of a document, or she said 'on condition that he returns her garments to me' and he accepted, she does not become prohibited, and it is required that he write the document. 'Wealth' or 'dirhams' means negation of existence of what she named, and on this there is no leniency at all, except that its requirement would be that if she named dirhams, and there were dinars in her hand, he is not owed other than dirhams, and I have not seen this. His saying: (And that is why he said in al-Qunyah) This phrase preceded shortly before his saying 'For if he divorces her by khul'.'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the rulings of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation) from the book Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the implications when the wife seeks khul' on unspecified property, such as saying 'on what is in my hand.' The key point is that if nothing is in her hand, she must return the dower she received; if she names a specific amount (like dirhams) and has less, she must complete it. However, if the compensation is not valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim), nothing is owed. The discussion then moves to gross ignorance (jahala mutafahisha), suggesting that the middle value should be imposed for known species. The passage also covers khul' on an absconding slave with a condition of immunity from guarantee—the condition is invalid, so the slave's value is due upon her if she cannot deliver the identical. It includes the ruling that khul' on the dower is valid, and if the wife had already absolved the husband of the dower, he cannot reclaim it. The commentator emphasizes that khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, unlike sale, as khul' is built on lenience. The text cites multiple sources like al-Kafi, Fath al-Qadir, al-Khaniya, al-Mabsut, al-Jawhara, and al-Qunyah to support the rulings. The overall message is that khul' is a flexible contract where the compensation need not be precisely specified, but certain limits apply to prevent gross ignorance or injustice.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation to the husband.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife at marriage; in khul', it may be returned.
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Property that has legal value and can be sold or exchanged for wealth.
جهالة متفاحشة (jahala mutafahisha): Gross ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter of a contract, leading to risk (gharar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife says to her husband: 'Divorce me by khul' on what is in my hand.' There is nothing in her hand. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The khul' is invalid because the compensation is unspecified.
B) The khul' is valid, and she owes nothing.
C) The khul' is valid, and she must return the dower she received.
D) The khul' is valid, and she must pay three dirhams as minimum compensation.

Key Takeaway
In khul' on unspecified property, if nothing exists, the dower is due; if named amount exists short, it must be completed; if grossly uncertain, the naming is invalid.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Khul' on Missing Property: The Absconding Slave and Void Conditions
Ruling with proof on khul' with missing property and void conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when a wife seeks khul' on an absconding slave with a condition of immunity from guarantee, and state the effect of void conditions in a khul' contract.

Core Idea
Khul' is built on lenience: a condition of immunity from guarantee for a missing item is void, but the khul' remains valid and she must deliver the item or pay its value.

This passage deals with a specific scenario: a wife offers an absconding slave as compensation in khul', stipulating that she is not liable if the slave is not found. The Hanafi position is clear: such a condition is void, but the khul' itself remains valid. This illustrates the broader principle that khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, unlike sale. Understanding this helps distinguish the flexible nature of khul' from ordinary sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The wife offers an absconding slave as compensation in khul', with a condition that she is free from guarantee if the slave is not delivered.
The slave is missing at the time of khul'.
2
Apply the principle of exchange
Khul' is a contract of exchange (mu'awada) that requires safety of the substitute. A condition of immunity contradicts this requirement, so it is a void condition (shart fasid).
The condition is void because it undermines the exchange nature.
3
Determine the consequence
The condition fails, but the khul' itself remains valid. The wife must deliver the slave if found, or pay its value if unable.
Khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, unlike sale where a void condition can invalidate the contract.
4
Compare with analogous cases
If the slave was dead before khul', the husband recourses for the dower (due to deception). If the slave dies after khul', the wife pays its value. Also, if the slave is later found to be the husband's property, nothing is due from her.
These distinctions show when value vs. dower applies.
5
Note the lenience of khul'
Naming an absconding slave is valid in khul' because its basis is lenience, unlike sale which requires precise delivery and would invalidate such a transaction due to gharar.
This is a key difference: khul' tolerates more uncertainty in the subject matter.

ما يثمر نخلها العام أو على ما في البيت من المتاع ولم يكن فيه شيء بطلت التسمية وردت ما سمي منه قبضت اهـ. وقيد بالخلع لأن السيد لو أعتق عبده على ما في يده من الدراهم وليس في يده شيء يجب عليه قيمة نفسه لو خالعها على عبد بعينه مثلاً وقد كان ميتاً قبل الخلع أنه يرجع عليها بالمهر الذي أخذته منه للغرور بخلاف ما لو مات بعده حيث تجب قيمته كما لو استحق وظهور حريته كموته قبل الخلع فيرجع عليها بالمهر قوله : ( وفيه نظر للجهالة المتفاحشة قال في النهر : ينبغي إيجاب الوسط في الكل وبه يندفع ما قال ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأن إيجاب الوسط في معلوم الجنس كالفرس والثوب الهروي بخلاف مجهول الجنس كالدابة والثوب ولذا لو سمى مهراً وجب مهر المثل قوله : وبهذا علم إن في كلام المصنف مسامحة الخ) قال في النهر : نفي الشيئية فيما إذا لم تسم له شيئاً معناه نفي الوجود، وفيها إذا سمت دراهم فإن خالع على عبد أبق لها على أنها بريئة من ضمانه لم تبرأ قالت طلقني ثلاثاً مهر عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف بقيمته لو كان عبداً كالمهر وقتله عنده بسبب كان عندها كاستحقاقه فيرجع بقيمته، كذا لو قطع يده؛ كذا في المبسوط. وأشار بقوله ردت المهر» إلى صحة الخلع على المهر وقد قال في الجوهرة وإن وقع الخلع على المهر صح فإن لم تقبضه المرأة سقط عنه وإن قبضته استرده منها .اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : خلعها بما لها عليه من المهر ظناً منه أن لها عليه بقية المهر ثم تذكر أنه لم يبق عليه شيء من المهر وقع الطلاق بمهرها فيجب عليها أن ترد المهر لأنه طلقها بطمع ما بقي عليه فلا يقع مجاناً، أما إذا علم أن لا لها عليه فلا شيء له اهـ. وفي القنية : ادعت مهرها على زوجها فأنكره ثم اختعلت نفسها بمهرها وقبل ثم تبين بالشهود أنها كانت أبرأته قبل الخلع فليس له شيء، ولو اختلعت على عبد ثم تبين أنه عبد الزوج ولا يعلم ذلك إلا بالتصادق ينبغي أن لا يلزمها شيء لأن ما هو بدل الخلع مسلم له كما لو علم أنه عبده قوله : (فإن خالعها على عبد أبق لها على أنها برية من ضمانه لم تبرأ لأنه عقد معاوضة فيقتضي سلامة العوض واشتراط البراءة شرط فاسد فبطل فكان عليها تسليم عينه إن قدرت وتسليم قيمته إن عجزت أشار إلى أن الخلع لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالنكاح ولذا قال في العمادي: لو خالعها على أن يمسك الولد عنده صح الخلع وبطل الشرط اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو اختلعت من زوجها على أن جعلت صداقها لولدها أو على أن تجعل صداقها لفلان الأجنبي قال محمد : الخلع جائز والمهر للزوج ولا شيء للولد ولا للأجنبي اهـ. ومعنى اشتراطها البراءة أنها إن وجدته سلمته وإلا فلا شيء عليها. وقيد باشتراط البراءة من ضمانه لأنها لو اشترطت البراءة من عيب في البدل صح الشرط وإنما صحت تسمية الآبق في الخلع لأن مبناه على المسامحة بخلاف البيع لأن مبناه على المضايقة، فالعجز عن التسليم يفضي إلى المنازعة فيه ولا كذلك هنا لأن العجز عن التسليم هنا دون العجز عن التسليم فيما إذا اختلعت على عبد الغير أو على ما في بطن غنمها وذلك جائز فكذا هنا وقيد بالشرط الفاسد لأن الشرط لو كان ملائماً لم يبطل ولذا قال في القنية : خالعها على ثوب بشرط أن تسلم إليه الثوب فقبلت فهلك الثوب قبل التسليم لم تبن لأنه يجعل نفس التسليم شرطاً. وهبت مهرها لأخيها فأخذ أخوها منه المهر قبالة ثم اختلعت نفسها منه بشرط أن تسلم إليه القبالة غداً فقبل ولم تسلم إليه القبالة غداً لم تحرم. ولو اختلعت بشرط الصك أو قالت بشرط أن يرد إليها أقمشتها فقبل لا تحرم ويشترط كتبه الصك مالاً أو دراهم معناه نفي وجود ما سمته وعلى هذا فلا مسامحة أصلاً إلا أن مقتضاه أنها لو سمت دراهم فإذا في يدها دنانير أنه لا يجب له غير الدراهم ولم أره. قوله : (ولذا قال في القنية تقدمت هذه العبارة قريباً قبيل قوله «فإن خالعها».
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. A group of dirhams is believed; the indication here is on the covenant, which is her saying 'on what is in my hand.' Thus in al-Kafi. And he clarified it in Fath al-Qadir, saying: Because her saying 'on what is in my hand' implies that the named thing is a container in her hand, and it is general, applying to dirhams and others, so dirhams became a covenant in general, because it is part of what the word 'what' indicates, which is vague. 'Min' was made an explication of it, and its object is the specific explanation of the container, and dirhams are an example. The intended meaning is that she explained the vague thing with a collective, like dinars. And it should be that her saying 'on what is in this house of sheep, horses, mules, or donkeys' is likewise, so three of the named thing are binding upon her. Then I saw in al-Mi'raj: but he added clothes, and there is a consideration due to gross ignorance. And he restricted by his saying 'and there is nothing in her hand', because if there was valuable wealth in her hand, it is for him, little or much, and she is not obligated to return the dower in the first case. As for the second, it is necessary that there be in her hand something of what she named. So if there was in her hand one or two dirhams, she must complete three. Thus in al-Khaniya and al-Mabsut. And by this it is known that there is a leniency in the author's words, because the absence of anything in her hand is a condition for returning the dower in the first case, and the absence of three is a condition in the second, and his words do not convey that. And his saying 'she returned the dower' indicates that it was received, so it indicates that if it had not been received, he is free of it and nothing is upon her, as al-Imadi mentioned in his chapters and in al-Jawhara. Then if recourse to the dower is due to him and she had absolved him, he does not recourse against her for anything, because the very thing he deserves has been delivered to him by the absolution; if he had recourse against her due to a gift, it does not obligate the donor a guarantee. End quote. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The conclusion is that if he returns what is not valuable, nothing is due; if he named an existing known thing, the named thing is due; if he named an unknown thing with remediable ignorance, likewise; and if the ignorance is gross and risk occurs, such as if he divorces her on what her palm tree yields this year or on what is in the house of goods and there is nothing in it, the naming is invalidated and she returns what she named and took of it. End quote. And it is restricted to khul' because if a master frees his slave on what is in his hand of dirhams and there is nothing in his hand, the value of himself is due upon him, because the usufruct of the vulva is not valuable at the time of exit, so it is not required that the named thing be known, unlike the slave, for he is valuable in himself, and unlike marriage, where the fair dower is due because it is valuable at the time of entry. Thus in al-Bada'i. And the first issue indicates that if he divorced her by khul' on a specific slave, for example, and it was dead before the khul', he recourses against her for the dower she took from him due to deception, unlike if he died after, where its value is due, just as if it was claimed and its freedom became apparent, like its death before khul', so he recourses against her for the dower. His saying: (And there is a consideration due to gross ignorance) He said in al-Nahr: It is appropriate to obligate the middle in all, and by it what he said is repelled. End quote. And there is a consideration because obligating the middle is for a known species like a horse and a Herawi cloth, unlike an unknown species like an animal and a cloth. That is why if he named a dower, the fair dower is due. His saying: And by this it is known that there is a leniency in the author's words, etc. He said in al-Nahr: The negation of thing-ness when she did not name him anything means negation of existence; and in it, if she named dirhams, then if he divorces her by khul' on an absconding slave of hers on condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free. She said: 'Divorce me three times for a dower' according to them, and according to Abu Yusuf, for its value if it was a slave like the dower, and his killing by him due to a cause while it was with her like its being claimed, he recourses for its value, similarly if he cut its hand. Thus in al-Mabsut. And his saying 'she returned the dower' indicates the validity of khul' on the dower, and he said in al-Jawhara: If khul' occurs on the dower, it is valid; if the woman did not take it, it falls from him; and if she took it, he retrieves it from her. End quote. And in al-Walwaljiyya: She divorced herself by khul' for the dower that he owed her, thinking that he owed her part of the dower, then he remembered that nothing of the dower remained upon him; the divorce occurs for her dower, so she must return the dower, because he divorced her with the hope of what remained upon him, so it does not occur for free. But if he knew that nothing was due to her, then nothing is due to him. End quote. And in al-Qunyah: She claimed her dower from her husband and he denied it, then she divorced herself by khul' for her dower, and he accepted, then it became clear by witnesses that she had absolved him before the khul'; he has nothing. And if she divorced herself by khul' on a slave, then it became clear that it was the husband's slave, and it is not known except by mutual confirmation, it is recommended that nothing be due from her, because what is the substitute of khul' is surrendered to him, just as if it was known that it was his slave. His saying: (For if he divorces her by khul' on an absconding slave of hers on condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free) because it is a contract of exchange, so it requires safety of the substitute, and stipulating freedom is a void condition, so it fails, so she must deliver its identical if able, and deliver its value if unable. He indicated that khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, like marriage. That is why he said in al-Imadi: If he divorces her by khul' on condition that he keep the child with him, the khul' is valid and the condition is void. End quote. And in al-Khaniya: If she divorced herself from her husband on condition that she made her dower for her child, or on condition that she made her dower for a stranger, Muhammad said: The khul' is valid and the dower is for the husband, and nothing for the child or the stranger. End quote. And the meaning of her stipulating freedom is that if she finds him, she delivers him; otherwise, nothing is upon her. And it is restricted by stipulating freedom from its guarantee, because if she stipulated freedom from a defect in the substitute, the condition is valid. And the naming of the absconding slave is valid in khul' because its basis is lenience, unlike sale because its basis is stringency. For inability to deliver leads to dispute in sale, not here, because inability to deliver here is less than inability to deliver when she divorces herself on a slave belonging to another or on what is in the belly of her sheep, which is permissible, so here likewise. And it is restricted by the void condition, because if the condition was appropriate, it would not be void. That is why he said in al-Qunyah: He divorced her by khul' on a cloth on condition that she deliver the cloth to him, and she accepted, then the cloth perished before delivery; she does not become prohibited, because he makes delivery itself a condition. And she gifted her dower to her brother, so her brother took from him the dower as a written promise, then she divorced herself by khul' on condition that she deliver the written promise to him tomorrow, and he accepted, and she did not deliver the written promise tomorrow; she does not become prohibited. And if she divorces herself by khul' with the condition of a document, or she said 'on condition that he returns her garments to me' and he accepted, she does not become prohibited, and it is required that he write the document. 'Wealth' or 'dirhams' means negation of existence of what she named, and on this there is no leniency at all, except that its requirement would be that if she named dirhams, and there were dinars in her hand, he is not owed other than dirhams, and I have not seen this. His saying: (And that is why he said in al-Qunyah) This phrase preceded shortly before his saying 'For if he divorces her by khul'.'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the rulings of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation) from the book Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the implications when the wife seeks khul' on unspecified property, such as saying 'on what is in my hand.' The key point is that if nothing is in her hand, she must return the dower she received; if she names a specific amount (like dirhams) and has less, she must complete it. However, if the compensation is not valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim), nothing is owed. The discussion then moves to gross ignorance (jahala mutafahisha), suggesting that the middle value should be imposed for known species. The passage also covers khul' on an absconding slave with a condition of immunity from guarantee—the condition is invalid, so the slave's value is due upon her if she cannot deliver the identical. It includes the ruling that khul' on the dower is valid, and if the wife had already absolved the husband of the dower, he cannot reclaim it. The commentator emphasizes that khul' is not invalidated by void conditions, unlike sale, as khul' is built on lenience. The text cites multiple sources like al-Kafi, Fath al-Qadir, al-Khaniya, al-Mabsut, al-Jawhara, and al-Qunyah to support the rulings. The overall message is that khul' is a flexible contract where the compensation need not be precisely specified, but certain limits apply to prevent gross ignorance or injustice.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation to the husband.
عبد آبق (abd abiq): An absconding slave (or any missing property) used as compensation in khul'.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A void condition that contradicts the nature of the contract.
مبناه على المسامحة (mabnahu 'ala al-musamaha): Its basis is lenience; refers to khul' being more flexible than sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife seeks khul' on an absconding slave, stipulating that she is not liable if the slave is not found. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The khul' is invalid because the slave is missing.
B) The khul' is valid, and the condition of immunity is also valid.
C) The khul' is valid, but the condition is void; she must deliver the slave or pay its value.
D) The khul' is valid, and she owes nothing because the condition protects her.

Key Takeaway
In khul', a condition of immunity from guarantee for a missing item is void, but the khul' remains valid; the wife must deliver the item or pay its value.

Talaq — Divorce

Khul‘ with an Invalid Condition: The Void Condition Does Not Void the Contract
Ruling on khul' with absconding slave and invalid condition of immunity from guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul‘ with an invalid condition and explain why the condition is void while the khul‘ remains valid.

Core Idea
In khul‘, a void condition does not nullify the contract; the khul‘ is valid and the condition is struck down.

The Hanafi school gives khul‘ a special flexibility: even if the wife imposes an invalid condition, the divorce-for-compensation remains valid. This lesson unpacks a specific case where the wife stipulates immunity from liability for an absconding slave. Understanding this ruling reveals a key principle: khul‘ is built on leniency, not strictness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the impugned condition
The case is khul‘ (divorce by compensation) where the wife gives an absconding slave as compensation and includes a condition that she is free from any guarantee (i.e., she is not liable if the slave is not found).
The condition is called 'barā’at min ḍamānih'.
2
Analyze khul‘ as a contract of exchange
Khul‘ is a commutative contract requiring sound consideration, meaning the compensation must be known and deliverable. The condition of immunity directly contradicts this requirement by negating liability.
This is unlike a gift, where conditions might be treated differently.
3
Determine the validity of the condition
Because the condition opposes the essential nature of exchange, it is deemed invalid (fāsid). The condition does not become effective, and the wife remains liable for the slave or its value.
The condition is void, not the entire contract.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle that invalid conditions do not void khul‘
Unlike in sale, where an invalid condition often voids the contract, in khul‘ (and marriage) the contract remains valid and only the condition is struck down. This is due to the leniency (musāmaha) underlying khul‘.
The same principle applies to conditions that are not inherently contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن خالعها على عبد أبق لها على أنها بريئة من ضمانه لم تبرأ لأنه عقد معاوضة فيقتضي سلامة العوض واشتراط البراءة شرط فاسد فبطل فكان عليها تسليم عينه إن قدرت وتسليم قيمته إن عجزت أشار إلى أن الخلع لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالنكاح ولذا قال في العمادي: لو خالعها على أن يمسك الولد عنده صح الخلع وبطل الشرط اهـ. وفي الخانية : لو اختلعت من زوجها على أن جعلت صداقها لولدها أو على أن تجعل صداقها لفلان الأجنبي قال محمد : الخلع جائز والمهر للزوج ولا شيء للولد ولا للأجنبي اهـ. ومعنى اشتراطها البراءة أنها إن وجدته سلمته وإلا فلا شيء عليها. وقيد باشتراط البراءة من ضمانه لأنها لو اشترطت البراءة من عيب في البدل صح الشرط وإنما صحت تسمية الآبق في الخلع لأن مبناه على المسامحة بخلاف البيع لأن مبناه على المضايقة، فالعجز عن التسليم يفضي إلى المنازعة فيه ولا كذلك هنا لأن العجز عن التسليم هنا دون العجز عن التسليم فيما إذا اختلعت على عبد الغير أو على ما في بطن غنمها وذلك جائز فكذا هنا وقيد بالشرط الفاسد لأن الشرط لو كان ملائماً لم يبطل ولذا قال في القنية : خالعها على ثوب بشرط أن تسلم إليه الثوب فقبلت فهلك الثوب قبل التسليم لم تبن لأنه يجعل نفس التسليم شرطاً. وهبت مهرها لأخيها فأخذ أخوها منه المهر قبالة ثم اختلعت نفسها منه بشرط أن تسلم إليه القبالة غداً فقبل ولم تسلم إليه القبالة غداً لم تحرم. ولو اختلعت بشرط الصك أو قالت بشرط أن يرد إليها أقمشتها فقبل لا تحرم ويشترط كتبه الصك مالاً أو دراهم معناه نفي وجود ما سمته وعلى هذا فلا مسامحة أصلاً إلا أن مقتضاه أنها لو سمت دراهم فإذا في يدها دنانير أنه لا يجب له غير الدراهم ولم أره .
If he divorced her (khul‘) in exchange for an absconding slave belonging to her, on condition that she is free from its guarantee, she is not free (from guarantee) because it is a contract of exchange, which requires the soundness of the consideration, and stipulating immunity is an invalid condition, so it becomes void. Thus, she must deliver the slave itself if she is able, and its value if she is unable. This indicates that khul‘ is not invalidated by invalid conditions, like marriage. Hence, al-‘Imādi said: If he gives her khul‘ on condition that he keep the child with him, the khul‘ is valid and the condition is void. In al-Khāniyyah: If a woman divorces herself (khul‘) from her husband on condition that she makes her dower for her child or for a certain stranger, Muḥammad said: The khul‘ is permissible, the dower belongs to the husband, and nothing belongs to the child or the stranger. The meaning of her stipulating immunity is that if she finds it, she delivers it; otherwise, she owes nothing. He restricted it to stipulating immunity from its guarantee because if she stipulates immunity from a defect in the consideration, the condition is valid. The naming of an absconding slave in khul‘ is valid because khul‘ is based on leniency, unlike sale, which is based on strictness. Inability to deliver leads to dispute in sale but not here, because inability to deliver here is like inability to deliver when she divorces for a slave belonging to another or for what is in the belly of her sheep, which is permissible. So likewise here. He restricted it to an invalid condition because if the condition is suitable, it does not void. Hence, in al-Qunyah: If he gives her khul‘ for a garment on condition that she delivers the garment to him, she accepts, then the garment perishes before delivery, she does not become unlawful (ḥarām) to him, because he makes delivery itself a condition. She gifted her dower to her brother, then her brother took from him the dower as a receipt, then she divorced herself from him on condition that she deliver the receipt tomorrow, he accepts, and she does not deliver the receipt tomorrow—she does not become unlawful. And if she divorces with the condition of a document, or she says on condition that he returns her garments, he accepts, she does not become unlawful, and it is required that he write the document as property or dirhams, meaning negation of the existence of what she named. On this, there is no leniency at all, except that its entailment is that if she names dirhams but she has dinars in her possession, that he is not owed other than the dirhams, and I have not seen this.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific case of khul‘ where the wife gives an absconding slave as compensation (badal) and stipulates that she is not liable for its guarantee. The ruling is that such a condition is invalid because khul‘ is a contract of exchange requiring the consideration to be sound and deliverable. The condition of immunity opposes this requirement, making it fāsid (defective). However, the khul‘ itself remains valid, and the wife must either deliver the slave or pay its value. The text draws a comparison with marriage, where invalid conditions do not nullify the contract. It further clarifies that if the condition is merely unsuitable but not contradictory to the contract’s nature, like stipulating a defect-free item, it can be valid. The rationale is that khul‘ is built on leniency (musāmaha), unlike sale which is strict. Therefore, even an absconding slave can be named as consideration in khul‘, and inability to deliver does not invalidate the contract. The passage also cites secondary rulings from al-‘Imādi, al-Khāniyyah, and al-Qunyah to illustrate that conditions that do not contravene the purpose of khul‘ are permissible, but conditions that aim to negate the very nature of exchange are void.

Key Terms
خلع (khul‘): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually the dower or an agreed amount).
عبد أبق (‘abd ābiq): A slave who has run away (absconding) and whose whereabouts are unknown.
براءة من ضمانه (barā’at min ḍamānih): Stipulation that the wife is free from liability for the slave, meaning she does not owe anything if it is lost or not found.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife gives khul‘ in exchange for a runaway slave and stipulates that she is free from its guarantee, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The khul‘ is void because the condition is invalid.
B) The khul‘ is valid and the condition is void; she must deliver the slave or its value.
C) Both the khul‘ and condition are valid because khul‘ is based on leniency.
D) She is free from liability and owes nothing.

Key Takeaway
An invalid condition in khul‘ is struck down, but the khul‘ itself remains valid; the wife must still fulfill the compensation.

Proportional Compensation in Khul‘ with Multiple Divorces
Ruling when wife asks for multiple divorces but husband gives fewer, including conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul‘ when a wife requests multiple divorces for a fixed compensation and the husband performs fewer, and apply the principle of proportional division of compensation based on the number of divorces actually executed.

Core Idea
In khul‘, compensation is divided proportionally to the number of divorces performed; the letter bā’ indicates compensation is tied to the object.

This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq addresses a common khul‘ scenario: the wife offers a sum for three divorces, but the husband gives only one. The Hanafi school rules that she owes one-third of the agreed amount. The reasoning is based on the linguistic function of the preposition ‘bā’’ and the principle that compensation is allocated according to the benefit received. The text also clarifies the importance of session continuity and the difference between wife-initiated and husband-initiated khul‘.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Determine the legal consequence when the wife requests three divorces but the husband performs one. The passage states he gets one-third and she becomes bā'in.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Proof
The preposition 'bā'' indicates compensation, and it is divided proportionally over the divorces. This is the primary dalil.
3
Understand the Session Condition
Divorce must occur in the same session; otherwise, no compensation is due. This is a condition for the khul‘ contract.
Contrast with husband-initiated khul' where wife's acceptance is in her own session.
4
Examine the Prior Divorce Scenario
If the wife had already been divorced twice, her request for a third commits her to the full amount because she seeks severe separation.
This shows the intention behind the request is critical.
5
Explore the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
For three separate pronouncements in one session, qiyās would give one divorce with one-third, but istiḥsān gives three with full compensation. The muftā bihī is istiḥsān.
This illustrates how istiḥsān overrides qiyās in certain cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت طلقني ثلاثاً بألف فطلق واحدة له ثلث الألف وبانت لأن الباء تصحب الأعواض وهو ينقسم على المعوض ويشترط أن يطلقها في المجلس حتى لو قام فطلقها لا يجب شيء، - كذا في فتح القدير ـ بخلاف ما إذا بدأ هو فقال خالعتك على ألف فإنه يعتبر في القبول مجلسها لا مجلسه حتى لو ذهب من المجلس ثم قبلت في مجلسها ذلك صح قبولها؛ كذا في الجوهرة أشار بطلبها الثلاث الى أنه لم يطلقها قبله إذ لو كان طلقها ثنتين ثم قالت طلقني ثلاثاً على أن لك ألف درهم فطلقها واحدة كان عليها كل الألف لأنها التزمت المال بإيقاع البينونة الغليظة وقد تم ذلك بإيقاع الثلاث؛ كذا في المبسوط والخانية. وينبغي أن لا فرق فيها بين الباء وعلى» لأن المنظور إليه حصول المقصود لا اللفظ ولذا قال في الخلاصة : لو قالت طلقني أربعاً بألف فطلقها ثلاثاً فهي بالألف، ولو طلقها واحدة فبثلث الألف اهـ. وقيد بكونه طلق واحدة إذ لو طلق الثلاث كان له الألف، جميع ،، سواء كان بلفظ واحد أو متفرقة بعد أن تكون في مجلس واحد ؛ كذا في فتح القدير لا يقال كيف وقع الثاني أن البائن لا يلحق البائن إلا إذا كان معلقاً لأنا نقول : قد أسلفنا أن مرادهم من البائن ما مع كان بلفظ الكناية لا مطلق البائن حتى صرحوا بوقوع أنت طالق ثلاثاً بعد البينونة . وفي التتارخانية : ثم في قولها طلقني ثلاثاً بألف إذا طلقها ثلاثاً متفرقة في مجلس واحد القياس أن تقع تطليقة واحدة بثلث الألف وتقع الأخريان بغير شيء، وفي الاستحسان تقع الثلاث
She said, ‘Divorce me three times for a thousand,’ and he divorced her once – he gets one-third of the thousand, and she becomes irrevocably divorced (bā’in). Because the letter bā’ accompanies compensations, and it is divided according to the compensated thing. It is conditional that he divorces her in the same session; if he stands up and divorces her, nothing is owed. Similarly, in Fatḥ al-Qadīr – unlike if he initiated and said, ‘I give you khul‘ for a thousand,’ then her acceptance is considered in her session, not his session, so if he left the session and then she accepted in her session, her acceptance is valid. Similarly, in al-Jawhara. Her request for three indicates that he had not divorced her before, because if he had divorced her twice, then she said, ‘Divorce me three times for a thousand dirhams,’ and he divorced her once, she would owe the entire thousand, because she committed to the money by effecting severe separation, and that was achieved by effecting three [divorces]. Similarly, in al-Mabsūṭ and al-Khāniyyah. It is appropriate that there is no difference between using the letter bā’ and the word ‘alā, because the goal is achieving the objective, not the wording. Hence, in al-Khulāṣah: If she said, ‘Divorce me four times for a thousand,’ and he divorced her three times, she owes the thousand; if he divorced her once, she owes one-third of the thousand. He restricted it to his divorcing once, because if he divorced three times, he gets the entire thousand, whether in one utterance or separate [utterances] as long as they are in one session. Similarly, in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. It cannot be said: How does the second divorce occur? Since the bā’in divorce cannot follow a bā’in divorce unless it is suspended. For we say: We have already stated that what they mean by bā’in is that which is with a kināyah wording, not absolute bā’in, as they explicitly stated that ‘You are divorced three times’ occurs after a bā’in divorce. In al-Tātārkhāniyyah: Then in her saying ‘Divorce me three times for a thousand,’ if he divorced her three times separately in one session, by qiyās one divorce occurs with one-third of the thousand, and the other two occur for nothing; by istiḥsān, three divorces occur.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a wife asks for three divorces in exchange for a thousand dirhams, but the husband performs only one divorce. The ruling is that the husband is entitled to one-third of the thousand, and the wife becomes bā’in (irrevocably divorced). The reasoning is that the preposition ‘bā’’ in ‘bi-alf’ is used for compensation, and the compensation is divided proportionally according to the number of divorces actualized. The text emphasizes that the divorce must occur in the same session; if the husband leaves the session and then divorces, no compensation is due. This is contrasted with the case where the husband initiates the khul‘; there, the wife’s acceptance is valid in her own session even if the husband has left. The passage also clarifies that if the wife had already been divorced twice, her request for three divorces implies she commits the entire thousand because she aims to achieve severe separation (baynūnah ghalīẓah), which is completed with three divorces. Furthermore, it notes that the distinction between using ‘bā’’ and ‘alā’ is irrelevant; both mean compensation. The text discusses the application of qiyās and istiḥsān regarding three separate pronouncements in one session: qiyās would result in only one divorce with one-third, while istiḥsān (the preferred position) results in all three divorces with full compensation.

Key Terms
باء (bā’): The Arabic letter ‘b’ used as a preposition for compensation in the phrase ‘bi-alf’ (for a thousand).
بائن (bā’in): A type of divorce that renders the wife irrevocably divorced (cannot be taken back without a new marriage).
قبول (qubūl): Acceptance by the wife in a khul‘ contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife says 'Divorce me three times for a thousand dirhams' and the husband divorces her once. How much is she obligated to pay?
A) 1000 dirhams.
B) 333⅓ dirhams (one-third).
C) Nothing.
D) 500 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
In khul‘, compensation is apportioned according to the number of divorces actually executed, and the session condition is critical for the wife's obligation.

Conditional Divorce via Agent: Dispute over Condition
First case from al-Khaniyya: conditional divorce via agent with dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates divorce with a suspending condition and later disputes its occurrence.

Core Idea
When divorce is conditioned, the husband's denial of the condition is accepted, and divorce does not occur.

This lesson examines the first scenario from the passage in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. A man appoints an agent to pronounce divorce on the condition that his wife does not take anything from the house. After the agent pronounces, the husband claims she took something. The ruling from al-Nawadir is that the husband's word is taken, and no divorce occurs. This is because he is denying the existence of the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Husbands Instruction
The husband said to the agent: 'Tell her: You are divorced if you do not take anything from the house.' This is a suspending condition (ta'liq).
The phrase 'if' (in) makes the divorce contingent on the condition.
2
Agent's Action
The agent conveyed the statement to the wife exactly as instructed.
3
Dispute Arises
The husband claims the wife took something; the wife denies it.
This is a dispute over the occurrence of the condition.
4
Apply the Ruling
The husband's word is accepted because he is the one denying the condition (munkir al-shart). He is not affirming the divorce, so his denial is preferred.
The principle: The one who denies the existence of a condition is believed over the one who affirms.
5
Conclusion
Divorce does not occur because the condition is not proven to have been breached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأقمشة في المجلس .اهـ. وفي الخانية : رجل قال لغيره طلق امرأتي على شرط أن لا تخرج من المنزل شيئاً فطلقها المأمور ثم اختلفا فقال الزوج إنها قد أخرجت من المنزل شيئاً، وقالت المرأة لم أخرج ذكر في النوادر أن القول قول الزوج ولم يقع الطلاق. قالوا: هذا الجواب صحيح إن كان الزوج قال للمأمور قل لها أنت طالق إن لم تخرجي من الدار شيئاً فقال لها المأمور ذلك ثم ادعى الزوج أنها قد أخرجت من المنزل شيئاً فيكون القول قوله لأنه منكر شرط الطلاق، أما إذا كان الزوج قال للمأمور قل لامرأتي أنت طالق على أن لا تخرجي من المنزل شيئاً فقال لها المأمور ذلك فقبلت ثم قال الزوج إنها قد أخرجت من المنزل شيئاً لا يقبل قوله لأن في هذا الوجه الطلاق يتعلق بقبول المرأة، فإذا قبلت يقع الطلاق للحال، أخرجت من المنزل شيئاً أو لم تخرج كما لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق على أن تعطيني ألف درهم فقالت قبلت تطلق للحال وإن لم تعطه ألفاً. وكذا لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق على دخولك الدار فقبلت تطلق للحال وإن لم تدخل الدار لأن كلمة «على» لتعليق الإيجاب بالقبول لا للتعليق بوجود القبول .اهـ. واستفيد من قوله لم تبرأ أن العقد يقتضي سلامة العوض فلذا في التتارخانية : لو قال لها أنت طالق غداً على عبدك هذا فقبلت وباعت العبد ثم جاء الغد يقع الطلاق وعليها قيمة العبد اهـ.
And the returning of fabrics in the sitting. End quote. And in al-Khaniyya: A man said to someone else: Divorce my wife on the condition that she does not take anything from the house, so the commanded one divorced her, then they disputed: the husband said she has taken something from the house, and the woman said I did not take. It is mentioned in al-Nawadir that the word is the husband's word, and the divorce did not occur. They said: This answer is correct if the husband said to the commanded one: Tell her: You are divorced if you do not take anything from the house, and the commanded one said that to her, then the husband claimed she took something from the house, so his word is taken because he is denying the condition of divorce. As for if the husband said to the commanded one: Tell my wife: You are divorced on condition that you do not take anything from the house, and the commanded one said that to her and she accepted, then the husband said she took something from the house, his word is not accepted, because in this case divorce is tied to the acceptance of the woman. When she accepts, divorce occurs immediately, whether she took something from the house or not, just as if he said to his wife: You are divorced on condition that you give me a thousand dirhams, and she said I accept, she is divorced immediately even if she does not give a thousand. Similarly, if he said: You are divorced upon your entering the house, and she accepts, she is divorced immediately even if she does not enter, because the word 'ala is for linking the offer to acceptance, not for linking to the occurrence of acceptance. End quote. And benefit is taken from his statement 'she is not free' that the contract necessitates soundness of the consideration, hence in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he said to her: You are divorced tomorrow on this slave of yours, and she accepted and sold the slave, then tomorrow comes, divorce occurs and she owes the value of the slave. End quote.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a citation from al-Khaniyya and al-Tatarkhaniyya, presenting rulings on delegated divorce (talaq bi'l-tawkīl) with conditions. The passage type is a ruling statement: it sets out the legal consequences when a man orders another to divorce his wife on a condition, and then a dispute arises about whether the condition was fulfilled. The key distinction is between two forms of expression: first, when the husband instructs the agent to say 'You are divorced if you do not take anything' (a conditional divorce, ta'liq), and second, when he instructs the agent to say 'You are divorced on condition that you do not take anything' and the wife accepts (a divorce tied to acceptance, tamlīk). In the first form, the divorce is contingent upon the condition; if the husband denies the occurrence of the condition, his word is taken as the one who negates the existence of the condition (since he is munkir al-shart). In the second form, the divorce occurs immediately upon acceptance, regardless of whether the condition is later fulfilled. The passage then generalises: whenever the word 'ala is used to link the offer (ījāb) to acceptance (qabūl), the divorce takes place at once, and the wife cannot stop it by failing to perform the condition. This is analogous to khul' where the wife accepts an offer of divorce for a consideration: the divorce occurs immediately and the consideration becomes binding. The passage concludes by noting that a contract of khul' requires the consideration to be sound; if the consideration perishes, the wife owes its value. All rulings here are from later fatwa collections (al-Khaniyya, al-Nawadir, al-Tatarkhaniyya), not from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
المأمور (al-ma'mūr): The person commanded by the husband to pronounce divorce on his behalf.
على (alā): Here used to indicate a condition that when accepted makes the divorce binding immediately, as opposed to a pure ta'liq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first form, what happens when the husband denies the condition?
A) His word is accepted and divorce does not occur.
B) The wife's word is accepted and divorce occurs.
C) The agent's testimony is required.
D) Divorce is suspended until evidence is presented.

Key Takeaway
When divorce is delegated with a suspending condition, the husband's denial of the condition is believed, and no divorce takes place.

Divorce on Acceptance: Immediate Effect
Second case from al-Khaniyya: divorce on condition with immediate effect upon acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why divorce occurs immediately when the husband uses 'on condition that' (tamlīk) and the wife accepts, and state that the condition is not a suspending factor.

Core Idea
When divorce is offered with 'on condition that' and accepted, the divorce is immediate; the condition becomes a binding obligation, not a suspension.

This lesson covers the second scenario from al-Khaniyya: the husband instructs the agent to say 'You are divorced on condition that you do not take anything from the house.' The wife accepts. Here, the divorce occurs at once, regardless of whether the wife later fulfills the condition. The key is the word 'ala (on condition that) which links the offer to acceptance, making the divorce effective immediately. This is analogous to khul' where the wife accepts a divorce for a consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Second Form
The husband says: 'Tell my wife: You are divorced on condition that you do not take anything from the house.' Note the phrase 'ala (on condition that).
This form is called tamlīk or i'jāb bi'l-'iwaḍ.
2
Agent Conveys and Wife Accepts
The agent tells the wife, and she accepts the offer.
Acceptance is required.
3
Effect of Acceptance
Upon acceptance, divorce occurs immediately, whether or not the condition is later fulfilled.
The condition is not suspending; it is a consideration ('iwaḍ) that becomes owed.
4
Analogous Cases
The passage gives examples: divorce for a thousand dirhams, or upon entering the house. In all, divorce is immediate.
The ruling is consistent: 'ala links ijab to qabul, not to occurrence.
5
Consequence: Consideration Owed
If the consideration perishes, the wife owes its value, as per the Tatarkhaniyya example.
The contract requires sound consideration.

Source Text

Key Takeaway
When divorce is offered with 'on condition that' and accepted, the divorce is immediate; the condition is not a suspending factor but a binding obligation.
